# big-code-kafka-bug-001: Kafka Producer Buffer Pool Reuse Race Condition

## Task

Investigate a bug in the Apache Kafka producer where a race condition in the `BufferPool` memory management causes messages to silently appear on the wrong topic. Trace the execution path from the producer's `send()` method through batch accumulation, buffer allocation, and network transmission to identify how buffer reuse can corrupt in-flight produce requests.

## Context

- **Repository**: apache/kafka (Java, ~800K LOC)
- **Category**: Bug Investigation
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Entry Point**: `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/producer/internals/Sender.java` — `sendProducerData()` and `failBatch()` methods

## Symptom

Users of the Kafka producer with non-zero `linger.ms` observe that messages published to topic A occasionally appear on topic B instead. The corruption is rare but occurs in bursts, typically during broker restarts or network disruptions. The CRC checksum on the records passes because it covers only key/value/headers, not the topic name. The produce request header (containing topic/partition) is serialized separately from the message payload.

The bug is a race condition: when an in-flight `ProducerBatch` expires or its broker disconnects, the batch's pooled `ByteBuffer` is returned to the `BufferPool` and immediately reused by a new batch — while the original batch's request is still being written to the network by the `Sender` thread.

## Requirements

1. Starting from the entry point, trace the execution path to the root cause
2. Identify the specific file(s) and line(s) where the bug originates
3. Explain WHY the bug occurs (not just WHERE) — focus on the buffer lifecycle
4. Propose a fix with specific code changes

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — examined for [reason]
- path/to/file2.ext — examined for [reason]
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Symptom observed in: path/to/symptom.ext
2. Called from: path/to/caller.ext (function name)
3. Bug triggered by: path/to/buggy.ext (function name, line ~N)
...

## Root Cause
- **File**: path/to/root_cause.ext
- **Function**: function_name()
- **Line**: ~N
- **Explanation**: [Why this code is buggy]

## Proposed Fix
```diff
- buggy code
+ fixed code
```

## Analysis
[Detailed trace from symptom to root cause, explaining each step]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- Root cause identification: Did you find the correct file(s) where the bug originates?
- Call chain accuracy: Did you trace the correct path from symptom to root cause?
- Fix quality: Is the proposed fix correct and minimal?

## Execution Flow Analysis

### 1. Normal Flow: `sendProducerData()` 

In `Sender.sendProducerData()`:
1. `accumulator.drain(...)` → calls `drainBatchesForOneNode()` → polls batches from deques → calls `batch.close()` (which calls `recordsBuilder.close()`, which calls `bufferStream.buffer().duplicate().flip()` to create the `builtRecords` slice)
2. `addToInflightBatches(batches)` → adds drained batches to `inFlightBatches` map
3. Expiry check for inflight batches (`getExpiredInflightBatches()`)
4. `accumulator.expiredBatches()` - expire undrained batches
5. `failBatch()` for expired batches
6. `sendProduceRequests(batches, now)` → calls `sendProduceRequest()` → builds ProduceRequest from `batch.records()` (= `recordsBuilder.build()` = just returns `builtRecords`) → sends via `client.send()`

### 2. `sendProduceRequest()` Key Detail

In `sendProduceRequest()`:
```java
for (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {
    TopicPartition tp = batch.topicPartition;
    MemoryRecords records = batch.records();  // returns builtRecords
    ...
    tpData.partitionData().add(new ProduceRequestData.PartitionProduceData()
            .setIndex(tp.partition())
            .setRecords(records));
    recordsByPartition.put(tp, batch);
}
```

The `MemoryRecords` object holds a reference to a `ByteBuffer.slice()` of the original buffer.

### 3. The `failBatch()` + `deallocate()` Path

When `failBatch()` is called:
1. `batch.completeExceptionally(...)` → marks batch as FAILED
2. `maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch(batch)` → `accumulator.deallocate(batch)` → `free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), batch.initialCapacity())`

`batch.buffer()` returns `recordsBuilder.buffer()` = `bufferStream.buffer()`.

1962026-03-11T18:33:51.356138agentmessage-
I am thinking...: This is extremely important! The `expandBuffer()` method in `ByteBufferOutputStream` can replace `this.buffer` with a NEW `ByteBuffer` when the original buffer runs out of space.

When this happens:
1. The original buffer (pool-allocated ByteBuffer) is essentially abandoned
2. A new, larger ByteBuffer is allocated via `ByteBuffer.allocate(expandSize)` (NOT from the pool)
3. `this.buffer` now points to the new, larger buffer

After `expandBuffer()`:
- `bufferStream.buffer()` returns the NEW larger buffer
- `bufferStream.initialCapacity()` returns the ORIGINAL buffer's capacity

So in `RecordAccumulator.deallocate(batch)`:
```java
free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), batch.initialCapacity());
```

- `batch.buffer()` = `recordsBuilder.buffer()` = `bufferStream.buffer()` = **the NEW expanded buffer** (not the pool-allocated one!)
- `batch.initialCapacity()` = `recordsBuilder.initialCapacity()` = `bufferStream.initialCapacity()` = the ORIGINAL capacity

Then in `BufferPool.deallocate(ByteBuffer buffer, int size)`:
```java
if (size == this.poolableSize && size == buffer.capacity()) {
    buffer.clear();
    this.free.add(buffer);
} else {
    this.nonPooledAvailableMemory += size;
}
```

- `size` = `initialCapacity` = ORIGINAL capacity = `poolableSize` (since the original buffer was poolable)
- `buffer.capacity()` = the NEW expanded buffer's capacity (which is LARGER than `poolableSize`)

So `size == this.poolableSize` is true, but `size == buffer.capacity()` is FALSE (because the new buffer is larger). Therefore, instead of adding the buffer to the free list, we just increment `nonPooledAvailableMemory`.

And the ORIGINAL pool-allocated buffer is **NEVER RETURNED TO THE POOL**. It's a memory leak!

This is the bug! When `ByteBufferOutputStream.expandBuffer()` is

3852026-03-11T19:22:05.714351agentmessage-
I am thinking...: 1301 main sources + 379 generated = 1680 source files to compile for main, plus 485 test sources. That's a lot.

Given 748MB available and the fact that an in-process compiler with -Xmx512m needs to compile 1680 Java files... let me think about this differently.

Java 17's javac with -Xmx512m can usually compile thousands of files if metaspace is sufficient. The issue in the previous runs was that the Gradle JVM itself was configured with insufficient memory and also doing in-process compilation.

The key insight: When compilation is NOT forked (`options.fork = false`), the compiler runs inside the Gradle JVM process. The Gradle JVM heap must accommodate BOTH Gradle's own working data AND the compiler's memory. This is what has been crashing.

When compilation IS forked (`options.fork = true`), the compiler runs in a CHILD process with its own JVM. The problem is both processes are alive simultaneously.

But actually, there might be a way out: **forked compilation is sequential**, not parallel! Gradle spawns the compiler JVM, waits for it to finish, then continues. The Gradle parent JVM is running but mostly idle during compilation (just waiting for the child to finish).

So total memory during forked compilation:
- Gradle JVM: mostly idle (GC can reclaim heap), real committed memory is much less than Xmx
- Compiler JVM: 0-512MB heap + 0-192MB metaspace

In practice, when the Gradle JVM is "idle" (waiting for forked process), its actual RSS is much lower than Xmx. Let's say Gradle JVM idles at ~128MB RSS.

So: 1300MB (base) + 128MB (idle Gradle) + 704MB (max compiler) = 2132MB... still over 2048MB.

Hmm, but Xmx is the MAXIMUM, not what's actually allocated. Java uses memory lazily:
- With -Xmx512m, the JVM doesn't allocate all 512MB upfront
- Actual h
... (truncated)

- Compiler JVM compiling 1680 files: maybe 200-400MB actual RSS

  "thought": "Now I understand the critical flow! Let me trace the execution path and identify the bug.\n\n## Execution Flow Analysis\n\n### 1. Normal Flow: `sendProducerData()` \n\nIn `Sender.sendProducerData()`:\n1. `accumulator.drain(...)` \u2192 calls `drainBatchesForOneNode()` \u2192 polls batches from deques \u2192 calls `batch.close()` (which calls `recordsBuilder.close()`, which calls `bufferStream.buffer().duplicate().flip()` to create the `builtRecords` slice)\n2. `addToInflightBatches(batches)` \u2192 adds drained batches to `inFlightBatches` map\n3. Expiry check for inflight batches (`getExpiredInflightBatches()`)\n4. `accumulator.expiredBatches()` - expire undrained batches\n5. `failBatch()` for expired batches\n6. `sendProduceRequests(batches, now)` \u2192 calls `sendProduceRequest()` \u2192 builds ProduceRequest from `batch.records()` (= `recordsBuilder.build()` = just returns `builtRecords`) \u2192 sends via `client.send()`\n\n### 2. `sendProduceRequest()` Key Detail\n\nIn `sendProduceRequest()`:\n```java\nfor (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {\n    TopicPartition tp = batch.topicPartition;\n    MemoryRecords records = batch.records();  // returns builtRecords\n    ...\n    tpData.partitionData().add(new ProduceRequestData.PartitionProduceData()\n            .setIndex(tp.partition())\n            .setRecords(records));\n    recordsByPartition.put(tp, batch);\n}\n```\n\nThe `MemoryRecords` object holds a reference to a `ByteBuffer.slice()` of the original buffer.\n\n### 3. The `failBatch()` + `deallocate()` Path\n\nWhen `failBatch()` is called:\n1. `batch.completeExceptionally(...)` \u2192 marks batch as FAILED\n2. `maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch(batch)` \u2192 `accumulator.deallocate(batch)` \u2192 `free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), batch.initialCapacity())`\n\n`batch.buffer()` returns `recordsBuilder.buffer()` = `bufferStream.buffer()`.\n\n`free.deallocate()` calls `buffer.clear()` and puts it back on the `free` deque!\n\n### 4. The Race Condition\n\nHere's the KEY problem:\n\n**Scenario 1: Batch completes + buffer is deallocated BEFORE the network layer sends it**\n\n```\nThread: Sender (I/O thread)\n\n1. sender.runOnce()\n   a. sendProducerData():\n      - drain() -> gets batch for tp0\n      - batch.close() -> creates builtRecords (a slice of buffer)\n      - addToInflightBatches(batches)\n      - [some batch expires]\n      - failBatch(expiredBatch) -> \n        - completeExceptionally()\n        - maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch() ->\n          - accumulator.deallocate(expiredBatch) ->\n            - free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), ...) ->\n              - buffer.clear()\n              - free.add(buffer)  // BUFFER IS NOW FREE!\n      - sendProduceRequests() -> sends drained batch with records\n   \n   b. client.poll() -> the data is transmitted\n\nBut wait...\n```\n\nActually wait. Looking more carefully at `sendProducerData()`:\n\n```java\n// create produce requests\nMap<Integer, List<ProducerBatch>> batches = this.accumulator.drain(metadataSnapshot, result.readyNodes, this.maxRequestSize, now);\naddToInflightBatches(batches);\n...\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredInflightBatches = getExpiredInflightBatches(now);\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredBatches = this.accumulator.expiredBatches(now);\nexpiredBatches.addAll(expiredInflightBatches);\n...\nfor (ProducerBatch expiredBatch : expiredBatches) {\n    ...\n    failBatch(expiredBatch, new TimeoutException(errorMessage), false);\n    ...\n}\n...\nsendProduceRequests(batches, now);\n```\n\nThe order is:\n1. `drain()` - batches are polled from deques and closed (finalized)\n2. `addToInflightBatches()` - add to in-flight map\n3. `getExpiredInflightBatches(now)` - check if any IN-FLIGHT batches have expired\n4. `accumulator.expiredBatches(now)` - check if any UNDRAINED batches expired\n5. `failBatch()` for ALL expired batches \u2192 this calls `maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch()` \u2192 deallocates buffer\n6. `sendProduceRequests()` - **AFTER** deallocation!\n\n**The bug is here**: Between step 5 and step 6:\n- A batch can be drained in step 1 AND it can be in `expiredInflightBatches` from a PREVIOUS send cycle (if `addToInflightBatches` from a previous iteration added it)\n- **OR**: Wait, in step 2, the freshly drained batches are added to inFlightBatches, then in step 3, `getExpiredInflightBatches` checks if any of them have expired. This is unlikely for freshly drained batches.\n\nWait, but the real issue might be different. Let me trace more carefully.\n\nActually, looking at the flow again:\n\n```java\nMap<Integer, List<ProducerBatch>> batches = this.accumulator.drain(metadataSnapshot, result.readyNodes, this.maxRequestSize, now);\naddToInflightBatches(batches);  // NOW batch is in inFlightBatches\n...\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredInflightBatches = getExpiredInflightBatches(now);\n```\n\nIf the delivery timeout has elapsed before the batch was sent (i.e., the batch sat in the accumulator too long), it might be detected as expired immediately after being drained and added to inFlightBatches. In that case:\n1. Batch is drained (`batch.close()` called, `builtRecords` slice created)\n2. Batch is added to `inFlightBatches`\n3. `getExpiredInflightBatches()` \u2192 finds batch has expired \u2192 returns it\n4. `failBatch()` \u2192 `completeExceptionally()` \u2192 **succeeds** (batch not yet \"done\" because `batch.isDone()` would be false before `completeExceptionally` is called)\n5. `accumulator.deallocate(batch)` \u2192 `free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), ...)` \u2192 `buffer.clear()` \u2192 buffer returned to free pool\n6. `sendProduceRequests(batches, now)` \u2192 uses `batch.records()` which is a SLICE of the now-cleared buffer!\n\n**THIS IS THE BUG!**\n\nBut wait... let me check `getExpiredInflightBatches()`:\n\n```java\nif (batch.hasReachedDeliveryTimeout(accumulator.getDeliveryTimeoutMs(), now)) {\n    iter.remove();\n    // expireBatches is called in Sender.sendProducerData, before client.poll.\n    // The !batch.isDone() invariant should always hold. An IllegalStateException\n    // exception will be thrown if the invariant is violated.\n    if (!batch.isDone()) {\n        expiredBatches.add(batch);\n    } else {\n        throw new IllegalStateException(batch.topicPartition + \" batch created at \" +\n            batch.createdMs + \" gets unexpected final state \" + batch.finalState());\n    }\n}\n```\n\nSo the check is `!batch.isDone()`. If the batch was just drained and is not done yet, it passes this check and gets added to expiredBatches.\n\nThen:\n```java\nfailBatch(expiredBatch, new TimeoutException(errorMessage), false);\n```\n\nIn `failBatch()`:\n```java\nif (batch.completeExceptionally(topLevelException, recordExceptions)) {\n    ...\n    maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch(batch);\n}\n```\n\n`batch.completeExceptionally()` will mark the batch as FAILED (done). Since the batch is not yet done, this succeeds. Then `maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch()` is called:\n\n```java\nprivate void maybeRemoveAndDeallocateBatch(ProducerBatch batch) {\n    maybeRemoveFromInflightBatches(batch);\n    this.accumulator.deallocate(batch);\n}\n```\n\n`accumulator.deallocate()`:\n```java\npublic void deallocate(ProducerBatch batch) {\n    incomplete.remove(batch);\n    if (!batch.isSplitBatch())\n        free.deallocate(batch.buffer(), batch.initialCapacity());\n}\n```\n\n`free.deallocate()`:\n```java\npublic void deallocate(ByteBuffer buffer, int size) {\n    lock.lock();\n    try {\n        if (size == this.poolableSize && size == buffer.capacity()) {\n            buffer.clear();  // <-- CLEARS THE BUFFER!\n            this.free.add(buffer);  // <-- RETURNS TO FREE POOL!\n        } else {\n            this.nonPooledAvailableMemory += size;\n        }\n        ...\n    } finally {\n        lock.unlock();\n    }\n}\n```\n\nNow, `batch.records()` returns `builtRecords` which is `MemoryRecords.readableRecords(buffer.slice())`.\n\nWhen `buffer.clear()` is called, it only resets the position/limit/mark of the ORIGINAL buffer. The `buffer.slice()` created in `MemoryRecordsBuilder.close()` is a separate `ByteBuffer` object with its own position/limit, but they **share the same underlying byte array** (the same backing array).\n\nSo after `buffer.clear()`, the slice (which is in `builtRecords`) still points to the same data, but **now a new batch can be allocated from that same buffer**! If a new batch is allocated from the free pool and starts writing new data to that buffer, it will **corrupt the `builtRecords` slice of the ORIGINAL batch**.\n\nThis is the race condition! But in the single-threaded Sender loop, all of these steps happen sequentially in the same thread iteration:\n\n1. `drain()` - batch closed, `builtRecords` slice created at position `[initialPosition, pos)`\n2. `addToInflightBatches()` - batch added to inFlightBatches\n3. `getExpiredInflightBatches()` - batch found to be expired and removed from inFlightBatches\n4. `failBatch()` - batch's buffer is deallocated, buffer.clear() called, buffer returned to free pool\n5. `sendProduceRequests()` - builds ProduceRequest using `batch.records()` which is `builtRecords`\n\nBut wait - does anything between steps 4 and 5 allocate from the free pool?\n\nLooking at `sendProduceRequest()`:\n```java\nfor (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {\n    TopicPartition tp = batch.topicPartition;\n    MemoryRecords records = batch.records();\n    ...\n```\n\n`batch.records()` is called here. Since `batch.close()` was called in `drain()`, `builtRecords` is already populated. The `builtRecords` holds a reference to a `ByteBuffer.slice()`. The position/limit of this slice is fixed when the slice was created. Even after `buffer.clear()` (which resets position/limit of the original buffer, not the slice), the slice still points to the correct region of the original buffer.\n\nActually wait - `buffer.clear()` only resets the position and limit of the `buffer` object. It does NOT zero out the data. And `builtRecords` is a slice of the buffer created at close time:\n\n```java\nByteBuffer buffer = buffer().duplicate();\nbuffer.flip();\nbuffer.position(initialPosition);\nbuiltRecords = MemoryRecords.readableRecords(buffer.slice());\n```\n\nSo `builtRecords` is a `ByteBuffer` that:\n- starts at position 0 (relative to the slice)\n- has limit = (pos - initialPosition) \n- shares the same backing array as the original buffer\n- but with its own position/limit\n\nAfter `buffer.clear()`, the original buffer has position=0 and limit=capacity. But the slice (`builtRecords`) still has position=0, limit=(original pos - initialPosition).\n\n**However**, if a new batch is allocated from the free pool and starts writing data at position 0, it will overwrite the data that `builtRecords` is pointing to!\n\nBUT - this write would only happen when an appender thread (producer thread) calls `RecordAccumulator.append()`, which calls `free.allocate()` and then creates a new batch. This is a different thread from the Sender thread. So the actual data corruption would be caused by a concurrent producer thread.\n\nThe sequence is:\n1. Sender thread: drain() \u2192 close batch \u2192 buffer holds records\n2. Sender thread: addToInflightBatches()\n3. Sender thread: failBatch() \u2192 deallocate \u2192 buffer.clear() \u2192 buffer returned to free pool\n4. Producer thread: allocate from free pool \u2192 gets same buffer \u2192 starts writing new records at position 0\n5. Sender thread: sendProduceRequests() \u2192 reads records from batch \u2192 **reads corrupted data!**\n\nWait, but this is the Sender sending a request using the FAILED batch's data? That doesn't quite make sense either, because if the batch is in the `batches` map that was drained in step 1, it's in `batches` and will be sent in `sendProduceRequests()`. Because the network layer's `ByteBuffer` is directly read during `client.send()` and potentially during `client.poll()` (when the actual I/O happens in `NetworkSend`). Can a batch be in `expiredInflightBatches` on the very same iteration that it was just drained?\n\nThe delivery timeout is checked in `hasReachedDeliveryTimeout()`:\n```java\npublic boolean hasReachedDeliveryTimeout(long deliveryTimeoutMs, long now) {\n    return this.createdMs + deliveryTimeoutMs <= now;\n}\n```\n\nIf `batch.createdMs + deliveryTimeoutMs <= now`, then YES, the batch is expired.\n\nFor a batch that was just created, this would only happen if `deliveryTimeoutMs <= 0` (which is possible in tests with `deliveryTimeoutMs = 0`) or if `now - createdMs >= deliveryTimeoutMs` (which happens for batches that sat in the accumulator too long).\n\nIn production, this could happen if:\n1. A record is appended, creating a batch\n2. The Sender is busy (lots of in-flight requests, network congestion) and doesn't drain the batch for a long time\n3. When the Sender finally drains the batch, the delivery timeout has already elapsed\n4. The batch gets drained and immediately expired in the same iteration\n\nNow, the fix: The fix should prevent the ProduceRequest from being sent for a batch that has already been failed/deallocated.\n\nOne approach: Before calling `sendProduceRequests()`, filter out any batches from `batches` that have been failed/deallocated. This could be done by checking `batch.isDone()`.\n\nAnother approach: Don't add newly drained batches to `inFlightBatches` and then immediately expire them. Instead, check if the batch has already expired BEFORE sending it.\n\nActually, looking at the code again more carefully:\n\n```java\n// create produce requests\nMap<Integer, List<ProducerBatch>> batches = this.accumulator.drain(metadataSnapshot, result.readyNodes, this.maxRequestSize, now);\naddToInflightBatches(batches);\nif (guaranteeMessageOrder) {\n    // Mute all the partitions drained\n    ...\n}\n\naccumulator.resetNextBatchExpiryTime();\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredInflightBatches = getExpiredInflightBatches(now);\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredBatches = this.accumulator.expiredBatches(now);\nexpiredBatches.addAll(expiredInflightBatches);\n\n...\nfor (ProducerBatch expiredBatch : expiredBatches) {\n    String errorMessage = \"Expiring \" + expiredBatch.recordCount + \" record(s) for \" + expiredBatch.topicPartition\n        + \":\" + (now - expiredBatch.createdMs) + \" ms has passed since batch creation\";\n    failBatch(expiredBatch, new TimeoutException(errorMessage), false);\n    ...\n}\n...\nsendProduceRequests(batches, now);\n```\n\nA batch can be in BOTH `batches` AND `expiredBatches`:\n- `batches`: freshly drained batch for tp0\n- `expiredInflightBatches`: the same batch (just added to inFlightBatches, now found expired)\n\nThe fix: In `sendProduceRequests()` (or before calling it), skip any batch that has been marked as done:\n\n```java\n// filter out batches that have been expired/failed\nfor (List<ProducerBatch> batchList : batches.values()) {\n    batchList.removeIf(ProducerBatch::isDone);\n}\nsendProduceRequests(batches, now);\n```\n\nOR alternatively, in `sendProduceRequest()`, check `batch.isDone()` and skip it.\n\nBut the deeper fix is to avoid sending data from a batch whose buffer has been returned to the pool. The cleanest fix is: **don't deallocate the batch's buffer until after the ProduceRequest has been queued for transmission**.\n\nActually, looking at it again: the simplest fix would be to move `sendProduceRequests()` BEFORE the expiry checks:\n\n```java\nMap<Integer, List<ProducerBatch>> batches = this.accumulator.drain(...)\naddToInflightBatches(batches);\n...\nsendProduceRequests(batches, now);  // SEND FIRST\n\n// THEN handle expiry\nList<ProducerBatch> expiredInflightBatches = getExpiredInflightBatches(now);\n...\n```\n\nBut this changes when the expiry is processed.\n\nActually, the simplest and most correct fix might be to not add the just-drained batches to `inFlightBatches` UNTIL after the expiry check. OR: move the expiry check for the just-drained batches after `sendProduceRequests()`.\n\nWait, but `getExpiredInflightBatches()` checks ALL batches in `inFlightBatches`, including those added by previous iterations. The issue is that by adding to `inFlightBatches` first, the freshly drained batches can be immediately found and expired.\n\nThe simplest fix that doesn't change the overall logic: **check `batch.isDone()` before sending in `sendProduceRequest()`**:\n\n```java\nprivate void sendProduceRequest(long now, int destination, short acks, int timeout, List<ProducerBatch> batches) {\n    if (batches.isEmpty())\n        return;\n\n    final Map<TopicPartition, ProducerBatch> recordsByPartition = new HashMap<>(batches.size());\n    ...\n    for (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {\n        if (batch.isDone()) continue;  // SKIP ALREADY-FAILED BATCHES\n        ...\n    }\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nActually, I think the proper fix is more subtle. Let me look at what the \"messages on wrong topic\" really means in the context of the buffer reuse.\n\nWhen `failBatch()` is called and `deallocate()` is called on the batch buffer:\n1. `buffer.clear()` resets position=0, limit=capacity\n2. `free.add(buffer)` adds it to the free list\n3. A subsequent `free.allocate()` call (from a producer appending to a DIFFERENT topic) returns this buffer\n4. The new batch writes its topic B data starting at position 0\n5. Meanwhile, the old batch's `builtRecords` (a slice of the same buffer) is being read by `sendProduceRequest()`\n\nIn single-threaded tests, steps 3-4 happen AFTER step 5 (since the producer thread and sender thread are separate threads). But in the test `testNoDoubleDeallocation`, everything happens on one thread, so step 5 is NOT corrupted.\n\nBut actually - even in single-threaded testing, the issue is:\n\nBetween `failBatch()` (which deallocates the buffer) and `sendProduceRequests()` (which creates the ProduceRequest), if another thread allocates that buffer and writes to it, the `records()` data will be corrupted.\n\nThis is fundamentally a use-after-free bug on the `ByteBuffer`.\n\nThe `MatchingBufferPool` test (testNoDoubleDeallocation) is checking that the buffer is not deallocated TWICE (which would be a different bug). The test passes because in the test scenario, the batch was already in-flight for a long time before expiry (it was sent in a previous runOnce() call, not the current one).\n\nLet me re-read the test:\n```java\nsender.runOnce();  // send request\nassertEquals(1, client.inFlightRequestCount());\nassertEquals(1, sender.inFlightBatches(tp0).size());\n\ntime.sleep(REQUEST_TIMEOUT);\nassertFalse(pool.allMatch());\n\nsender.runOnce();  // expire the batch\n```\n\nIn the second `sender.runOnce()`:\n1. `sendProducerData()`:\n   - `drain()` - empty (batch already drained)\n   - `addToInflightBatches({})` - nothing new\n   - `getExpiredInflightBatches(now)` - finds the batch (already in inFlightBatches from first runOnce) \u2192 removes it, adds to expiredBatches\n   - `failBatch()` \u2192 deallocated\n   - `sendProduceRequests({})` - empty map, nothing to send\n2. `client.poll()`:\n   - MockClient processes the timed-out request \u2192 `handleProduceResponse()` \u2192 batch already done \u2192 no-op\n\nSo in this test, `batches` (from drain) is empty, and the expired batch was one that was already in-flight from a previous iteration. There's NO scenario where the expired batch is in BOTH `batches` (current drain) and `expiredInflightBatches`.\n\nSo the bug is specifically:\n**When a batch is both drained in the current iteration AND immediately expired in the same `sendProducerData()` call, it gets failed+deallocated BEFORE `sendProduceRequests()` can transmit it. If a concurrent producer thread then reuses the buffer for a different topic, the data sent to the broker is corrupted.**\n\nBut the test `testNoDoubleDeallocation` doesn't test this specific scenario.\n\nNow let me think about what the actual failing test is and why it's failing. Write solution.md\n\nActually, let me also look at what happens in `sendProduceRequests()` when `batches` is non-empty but some batches have been marked as done. If the buffer of batch_A was reused for batch_B (tp1, say), and the broker received the corrupted data as a ProduceRequest for tp0, the response comes back for tp0, and `recordsByPartition.get(tp0)` returns `batch_A`. But `batch_A` is already FAILED, so the response processing is a no-op.\n\nThe actual messages that went to the broker are from the buffer of batch_B (which was written after reuse). The broker interprets these as messages for tp0 (from the ProduceRequest framing). So messages meant for tp1 end up on tp0 - or more precisely, the raw bytes of batch_B (which contain batch_B's metadata/records) are sent as if they were for tp0.\n\nActually, the broker would likely reject this as an invalid record batch (since the batch header was written by batch_B for a different topic/magic/sequence), causing an error response. "public ByteBufferOutputStream(int initialCapacity, boolean directBuffer) {\n        this(directBuffer ? {
  "thought": "This is extremely important! The `expandBuffer()` method in `ByteBufferOutputStream` can replace `this.buffer` with a NEW `ByteBuffer` when the original buffer runs out of space.\n\nWhen this happens:\n1. The original buffer (pool-allocated ByteBuffer) is essentially abandoned\n2. A new, larger ByteBuffer is allocated via `ByteBuffer.allocate(expandSize)` (NOT from the pool)\n3. `this.buffer` now points to the new, larger buffer\n\nAfter `expandBuffer()`:\n- `bufferStream.buffer()` returns the NEW larger buffer\n- `bufferStream.initialCapacity()` returns the ORIGINAL buffer's capacity\n\nSo in `RecordAccumulator.deallocate(batch)`:\n```java\nfree.deallocate(batch.buffer(), batch.initialCapacity());\n```\n\n- `batch.buffer()` = `recordsBuilder.buffer()` = `bufferStream.buffer()` = **the NEW expanded buffer** (not the pool-allocated one!)\n- `batch.initialCapacity()` = `recordsBuilder.initialCapacity()` = `bufferStream.initialCapacity()` = the ORIGINAL capacity\n\nThen in `BufferPool.deallocate(ByteBuffer buffer, int size)`:\n```java\nif (size == this.poolableSize && size == buffer.capacity()) {\n    buffer.clear();\n    this.free.add(buffer);\n} else {\n    this.nonPooledAvailableMemory += size;\n}\n```\n\n- `size` = `initialCapacity` = ORIGINAL capacity = `poolableSize` (since the original buffer was poolable)\n- `buffer.capacity()` = the NEW expanded buffer's capacity (which is LARGER than `poolableSize`)\n\nSo `size == this.poolableSize` is true, but `size == buffer.capacity()` is FALSE (because the new buffer is larger). Therefore, instead of adding the buffer to the free list, we just increment `nonPooledAvailableMemory`.\n\nAnd the ORIGINAL pool-allocated buffer is **NEVER RETURNED TO THE POOL**. It's a memory leak!\n\nThis is the bug! When `ByteBufferOutputStream.expandBuffer()` is called during record appending, the original pool buffer is replaced with a larger one. When `RecordAccumulator.deallocate(batch)` is called, it attempts to return `batch.buffer()` (the NEW larger buffer) to the pool. But since the buffer's capacity doesn't match `poolableSize`, it only increments `nonPooledAvailableMemory`. The original pool buffer is leaked.\n\nBUT WAIT - the `MatchingBufferPool` tracks buffers that were ALLOCATED via `allocate()`. The original pool buffer WAS in `allocatedBuffers`. But the EXPANDED buffer was NOT (it was allocated via `ByteBuffer.allocate()`, not via `MatchingBufferPool.allocate()`).\n\nWhen `MatchingBufferPool.deallocate(expanded_buffer, size)` is called:\n```java\nif (!allocatedBuffers.containsKey(buffer)) {\n    throw new IllegalStateException(\"Deallocating a buffer that is not allocated\");\n}\n```\n\nThe `expanded_buffer` was NOT allocated via `MatchingBufferPool.allocate()`, so it's NOT in `allocatedBuffers`. This throws `IllegalStateException(\"Deallocating a buffer that is not allocated\")`!\n\n**This is the bug that `MatchingBufferPool` catches!**\n\nSo when records are compressed and the compression output exceeds the original buffer's capacity, `ByteBufferOutputStream.expandBuffer()` is called, creating a new buffer. When the batch is later deallocated, `batch.buffer()` returns the EXPANDED buffer, but `MatchingBufferPool` throws because the expanded buffer was never \"allocated\" from the pool.\n\nBut wait - what about the part of the `ByteBufferOutputStream` code in `expandBuffer()`:\n```java\nbuffer.position(initialPosition);\nbuffer = temp;\n```\n\nThe original buffer's position is reset to `initialPosition`. The original buffer is abandoned (held by nobody except maybe some existing reference). Run tests\n\nLet me consolidate what I know about the bug:\n\n**Bug 1: Use-After-Free / Buffer Reuse Before Send**\n- In `sendProducerData()`, a batch is drained, added to inFlightBatches, then immediately found to be expired (if delivery timeout elapsed while batch was in accumulator)\n- The batch is failed and its buffer is returned to the pool\n- `sendProduceRequests()` is STILL called with the failed batch (it's in `batches` from `drain()`)\n- The ProduceRequest uses `batch.records()` which references the now-freed buffer\n- Concurrent producers may reuse the buffer, causing data corruption\n\n**Bug 2: Buffer Identity Mismatch When ByteBufferOutputStream Expands**\n- When records are appended and the buffer runs out of space, `ByteBufferOutputStream.expandBuffer()` allocates a new, larger buffer\n- The original pool-allocated buffer is abandoned\n- When the batch is deallocated, `batch.buffer()` returns the EXPANDED buffer (not the pool buffer)\n- The pool doesn't recognize the expanded buffer \u2192 either throws or just increments `nonPooledAvailableMemory`\n- The original pool buffer is permanently leaked\n\nFor Bug 2, the `MatchingBufferPool` would throw `IllegalStateException(\"Deallocating a buffer that is not allocated\")`.\n\nNow, which bug is more likely to cause \"messages appear on wrong topic\"?\n\nBug 1: The batch is sent with (potentially corrupted) data. If the buffer is reused by a producer writing to TOPIC B while the Sender is sending TOPIC A's ProduceRequest, the bytes transmitted correspond to TOPIC B's records but are framed as TOPIC A's request. The broker would receive TOPIC A's ProduceRequest with TOPIC B's raw bytes. This would likely cause a parsing error or the broker would store corrupted records under TOPIC A. In extreme cases, if the data happens to be valid for TOPIC A, the messages appear correct but are actually TOPIC B's data. This scenario doesn't directly cause TOPIC B messages to appear on TOPIC A (since the TOPIC B batch is also sent separately when the producer writes it).\n\nBug 2: This is a memory leak. Doesn't directly cause messages to appear on wrong topic.\n\nActually, let me think about \"wrong topic\" more carefully with Bug 1:\n\nWhen `ByteBufferOutputStream.expandBuffer()` creates B2 and abandons B1:\n- B1 is in `BufferPool.free` deque? NO! B1 was allocated from the pool (removed from `free`). After `expandBuffer()`, B1 is still \"checked out\" from the pool (since it was removed from `free` when allocated). But nobody has a reference to B1 anymore (the batch's `bufferStream.buffer` changed to B2, and `RecordAccumulator.append()` had `buffer = null`).\n\nActually wait - in `RecordAccumulator.append()`:\n```java\nByteBuffer buffer = free.allocate(size, maxTimeToBlockMs);\n...\n// Create new batch\nMemoryRecordsBuilder recordsBuilder = recordsBuilder(buffer, magic);\nProducerBatch batch = new ProducerBatch(tp, recordsBuilder, nowMs);\n...\nbuffer = null;  // set to null so it doesn't get freed in finally\n...\n} finally {\n    if (buffer != null)\n        free.deallocate(buffer);\n}\n```\n\nSo `buffer` is set to null after the batch is created. The batch's `recordsBuilder` \u2192 `bufferStream` holds the reference to B1 initially.\n\nIf during record appending, `expandBuffer()` is called:\n- `bufferStream.buffer` = B2\n- `bufferStream.initialCapacity` = B1.capacity() = batchSize\n- B1 is no longer referenced by `bufferStream`\n- B1 is essentially garbage-collectable (no references)\n\nBut from the pool's perspective, B1 was allocated (removed from `free` deque). The pool's `availableMemory` was decremented by batchSize when B1 was allocated. When `deallocate(B2, batchSize)` is called:\n- `B2.capacity() > batchSize`, so the pool's conditions fail\n- `nonPooledAvailableMemory += batchSize`\n\nSo the pool THINKS it got back batchSize bytes (via `nonPooledAvailableMemory`), but the ACTUAL pool buffer (B1) is leaked (never returned to `free` deque). Over many such expansions, `nonPooledAvailableMemory` keeps increasing but `free` deque is empty, leading to B1 objects being GC'd but the pool losing track.\n\nActually, hold on. When B1 is allocated from the pool:\n- `free.remove()` removes B1 from the deque\n- `totalMemory` stays the same\n- `availableMemory` = `totalMemory - allocatedUnpooled` is implicit\n\nWhen `deallocate(B2, batchSize)` is called (where B2 != B1 and B2.capacity() != batchSize):\n- `nonPooledAvailableMemory += batchSize`\n\nBut the actual batchSize memory from B1 is NOT tracked (B1 is GC'd). So the pool's accounting shows batchSize memory is available (via `nonPooledAvailableMemory`), but the actual B1 ByteBuffer is lost. When the pool later tries to allocate `batchSize` bytes from `nonPooledAvailableMemory`, it can successfully do so (allocate a new ByteBuffer). No actual problem with memory accounting per se (since B1 is GC'd, it doesn't waste system memory).\n\nBUT the pool-reusable buffer B1 is LOST. It cannot be returned to the `free` deque for future pool reuse. This is a performance issue (more allocations instead of pool reuse).\n\nThe `MatchingBufferPool` catches the IDENTITY mismatch: B2 was never in `allocatedBuffers`, so `deallocate(B2, ...)` throws.\n\nNow, could this cause \"messages appear on wrong topic\"? The fix is to NOT send a ProduceRequest for a batch that has been already failed (isDone).\n\nIn `sendProduceRequest()`, add:\n```java\nfor (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {\n    if (batch.isDone()) continue;  // Skip already-completed/failed batches\n    ...\n}\n```\n\nThis ensures that if the batch was failed (and its buffer returned to the pool) before `sendProduceRequests()` is called, we simply don't include it in the request.\n\nBut wait - there's a subtler issue. Even if we skip the batch in the ProduceRequest, the buffer is ALREADY in the pool's free deque, and a producer thread might be concurrently writing to it. The ProduceRequest correctly doesn't include this batch, but there's still no crash (the data corruption would only happen if we sent the data).\n\nWith the fix, the use-after-free is prevented because we don't read from the buffer after it's been returned to the pool.\n\nLet me also think about whether there are other threads that could cause issues:\n- The `client.send()` operation in `sendProduceRequest()` creates a `ClientRequest` which references the `MemoryRecords` (which references `builtRecords` = a slice of the pool buffer).\n- `client.poll()` reads from this `MemoryRecords` to write data to the socket.\n- If the buffer is in the pool when `client.poll()` reads it... but with the fix, we never add the batch to the request, so `client.poll()` doesn't read from its buffer.\n\nThe fix is:\n```java\nfor (ProducerBatch batch : batches) {\n    if (batch.isDone()) continue;\n    ...\n}\nif (recordsByPartition.isEmpty()) return;\n```\n\nThis is the minimal correct fix.\n\nNow let me also think about whether we need to handle the case where the batch is PARTIALLY skipped. If node N has batches [B1 (tp0), B2 (tp1)], and B1 is done but B2 is not, we should still send a ProduceRequest for B2. With the fix, B1 is skipped and B2 is sent. That's correct.\n\nIf ALL batches for a node are done, `recordsByPartition.isEmpty()` would be true, and we return without sending anything. The node was included in `batches` but we skip it entirely.\n\nThis is a good fix. Let me now also verify: is there a case where NOT checking `batch.isDone()` causes the double-deallocation that `testNoDoubleDeallocation` checks for?\n\nActually, the test `testNoDoubleDeallocation` doesn't test the scenario where the batch is drained and immediately expired. It tests the scenario where the batch was ALREADY IN-FLIGHT before expiry.\n\nBut with the fix, if we add `if (batch.isDone()) continue;` in `sendProduceRequest()`, would `testNoDoubleDeallocation` still pass? Yes, because in that test:\n- First runOnce: batch drained \u2192 NOT yet expired \u2192 sent \u2713\n- Second runOnce: no new drain \u2192 getExpiredInflightBatches \u2192 failBatch \u2192 dealloc \u2192 sendProduceRequests({}) \u2192 nothing to send\n- Nothing changes about the double-deallocation path\n\nSo the fix doesn't break `testNoDoubleDeallocation`.\n\nNow, let me also think about what happens when a batch is sent (step 7) and then the response comes back (step 8) - would there be a second deallocation?\n\nWith the fix: the batch is NOT sent (we skip done batches). But the `client.send()` was never called, so there's no pending request and no response callback. The batch was already failed and deallocated. No second deallocation.\n\nWait, but in the scenario I described, the batch IS in `batches` (from `drain()`). After the fix, we skip it in `sendProduceRequest()`. The batch doesn't become part of any `recordsByPartition` map. No response callback is registered for this batch. The batch's futures are already resolved (with TimeoutException). The user already got their error. The buffer was already deallocated. Everything is clean.\n\nNow let me think about the mock client in tests. In `testNoDoubleDeallocation`:\n- First runOnce: batch is sent \u2192 `client.inFlightRequestCount() = 1`\n- Second runOnce: batch expires \u2192 deallocated \u2192 `client.poll()` handles the timeout \u2192 `client.inFlightRequestCount() = 0`\n- Third runOnce: nothing \u2192 `client.inFlightRequestCount() = 0` \u2713\n\nWith the fix, this doesn't change. The batch was already sent in the first runOnce. The fix only prevents sending a batch that was drained AND immediately expired in the SAME iteration. That doesn't happen in this test.\n\nOK so now I'm fairly confident:\n\n**The Bug**: In `Sender.sendProducerData()`, when a batch is drained from the accumulator AND found to be expired in the same iteration:\n1. `drain()` drains the batch\n2. `addToInflightBatches()` adds it to in-flight tracking\n3. `getExpiredInflightBatches()` finds it expired, removes it from in-flight tracking\n4. `failBatch()` marks it FAILED and returns its buffer to the `BufferPool`\n5. `sendProduceRequests()` STILL includes this batch in the ProduceRequest\n\nStep 5 is a use-after-free: the batch's buffer has been returned to the pool. A concurrent producer thread could reuse this buffer for a different topic, causing the ProduceRequest for the original topic to contain the other topic's data.\n\n**The Fix**: In `sendProduceRequest()`, skip batches that have already been marked as done (`batch.isDone()`).\n\nNow let me implement the fix and write solution.md.\n"
}
